    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1371675044" />
    <a title="Jason Koivu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu"><img alt="Jason Koivu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560521450p2/1742824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1371675044?book_show_action=true">Aug 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Koivu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Koivu" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu">Jason Koivu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393343069" href="/book/show/13707721-uncle-fred-in-the-springtime">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=comedy">comedy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=wodehouses">wodehouses</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=humor">humor</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1371675044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11228124442533754793">Oh my goodness, what in the dickens is going on now? Impostors, you say? At Blandings Castle, you say? Well, you don't say!<br/><br/>Lord Ickenham, aka Uncle Fred is at it again. The OCC (original cool cucumber) has cooked up another improbable scheme to make all well again in a world in which he loves her, she loves him, Father A doesn't approve, Father B doesn't approve, Young Gadabout A needs a bit of the ready cash, and so does Young Gadabout B. Who better to tie these things all together than Uncle F</span>
  <span id="freeText11228124442533754793" style="display:none">Oh my goodness, what in the dickens is going on now? Impostors, you say? At Blandings Castle, you say? Well, you don't say!<br /><br />Lord Ickenham, aka Uncle Fred is at it again. The OCC (original cool cucumber) has cooked up another improbable scheme to make all well again in a world in which he loves her, she loves him, Father A doesn't approve, Father B doesn't approve, Young Gadabout A needs a bit of the ready cash, and so does Young Gadabout B. Who better to tie these things all together than Uncle Fred?<br /><br />Wodehouse juggles plots with dizzying skill. I did a rough count and <i>Uncle Fred in the Springtime</i> contains approximately a bucketload of characters. Every character's got an agenda and they all compete with and against one another simultaneously. Sometimes the plot lines are silly, sometimes skillful, and sometimes they leave you wondering, "What? Who? Where?" in the most delightful way. It's like a murder mystery in which no one gets murdered...not too seriously at any rate. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11228124442533754793" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_962927863" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/962927863" />
    <a title="Mark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4339777-mark"><img alt="Mark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1295487181p2/4339777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/962927863?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark" href="/user/show/4339777-mark">Mark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393343069" href="/book/show/13707721-uncle-fred-in-the-springtime">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer962927863" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9327346223764196905">Time was I read a lot of PG Wodehouse, in the pre-web days where series that were oversupplied in used bookstore had a special place in my heart. I eventually had to stop because while I loved them I couldn't remember if I'd read <i>Carry On, Jeeves</i> but not <i>Right Ho, Jeeves</i>. And since all the plots involved Bertie starting himself accidentally engaged while trying to help out a friend and ended with him giving up on his desire to wear a green tie or white pants over the objections of Jeeves, I simp</span>
  <span id="freeText9327346223764196905" style="display:none">Time was I read a lot of PG Wodehouse, in the pre-web days where series that were oversupplied in used bookstore had a special place in my heart. I eventually had to stop because while I loved them I couldn't remember if I'd read <i>Carry On, Jeeves</i> but not <i>Right Ho, Jeeves</i>. And since all the plots involved Bertie starting himself accidentally engaged while trying to help out a friend and ended with him giving up on his desire to wear a green tie or white pants over the objections of Jeeves, I simply ran out of options. (A friend implied you could remember which book it was based on which piece of clothing Bertie sacrificed at the end. If there's not already an app for that, there should be.) <br /><br />After twenty-five years later it seemed OK to start reading again, since the plots are interchangeable anyway and I certainly don't remember the jokes anymore. This book is from the Blandings series so a different cast of characters. Uncle Fred is an interesting sort--he's a sixty year old Earl with the recklessness, overconfidence and generosity of Wooster with something approaching the intelligence and savoir-faire of Jeeves. The rest are 20-somethings who got a little confusing, since it's too easy to think of Fred as <i>everyone's</i> uncle the romantic triangles get a little weird.<br /><br />I'll make one slightly serious point: In my twenties the 'old ball and chain' stereotype of all women over 50 must have passed by unnoticed, but it got old this time around. One of those annoying period jokes that just didn't age well, especially when the nominally good-hearted hero indulges in quips that come off a bit mean-spirited today. The book could have used an Aunt Dahlia.<br /><br />Stats for the PG Wodehouse app I mentioned:<br /><br />Plot Keyword: Pignapping, <br />Number of brain specialists: 2<br />Number of non-impostor brain specialists: 1<br />Primary card game: Persian monarchs<br />Best phobia: Fear of lambs<br />Lost clothing: None. This is a Blanding book, not a Jeeves book. Do pay attention.<br />Worth Reading: Yes</span>
  <a data-text-id="9327346223764196905" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6347896546368625468"><b>"It is always my aim to try and spread sweetness and light."</b><br/><br/>This is Wodehouse at his absolutely best! My favorite book of his to date! <br/><br/>Lord "Call me Uncle Fred" Ickenham, a loveable gentleman in his sixties who feels like he is still in his twenties, comes to life the second his wife leaves for France, giving him strict orders to stay at home and out of trouble. But what's a meddling uncle to do when his favorite nephew manages to cause his fiancé to break off the engagement, said nephew's best </span>
  <span id="freeText6347896546368625468" style="display:none"><b>"It is always my aim to try and spread sweetness and light."</b><br /><br />This is Wodehouse at his absolutely best! My favorite book of his to date! <br /><br />Lord "Call me Uncle Fred" Ickenham, a loveable gentleman in his sixties who feels like he is still in his twenties, comes to life the second his wife leaves for France, giving him strict orders to stay at home and out of trouble. But what's a meddling uncle to do when his favorite nephew manages to cause his fiancé to break off the engagement, said nephew's best friend manages to anger his fiancé (who so happens to be Uncle Fred's favorite niece), and people left and right are begging him to set various things to rights? What follows is a hilarious romp which includes a pig-napping, Fred impersonating the respectable looney doctor, ehm, nerve specialist, Roderick Glossop, mix-ups galore, and, of course, happy endings for everyone. <br /><br />To use the blurb Stephen Fry wrote for the Wodehouse novels: <br /><br /><b>"You don't analyze such sunlit perfection, you just bask in its warmth and splendor."</b><br /><br />I need to get my hands on more Uncle Fred stories!!!</span>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/827746249?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bokeshi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bokeshi" href="/user/show/19587199-bokeshi">Bokeshi</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19587199-bokeshi?shelf=owned-books">owned-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19587199-bokeshi?shelf=humor-comedy">humor-comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer827746249" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15144577320230548432">Once again Wodehouse brings roses back to the cheeks. And once again I am amazed by his ability with words, aided by his astounding vocabulary and apparent erudition. From beautifully worded original descriptions in polysyllables, to both popular and obscure Latinisms, to numerous vernaculars and slang, he seems perfectly at home with them all and can fashion them into something light, bright and sparkling. His command of the English language is gorgeously beautiful, and as Keats puts it, "A thi</span>
  <span id="freeText15144577320230548432" style="display:none">Once again Wodehouse brings roses back to the cheeks. And once again I am amazed by his ability with words, aided by his astounding vocabulary and apparent erudition. From beautifully worded original descriptions in polysyllables, to both popular and obscure Latinisms, to numerous vernaculars and slang, he seems perfectly at home with them all and can fashion them into something light, bright and sparkling. His command of the English language is gorgeously beautiful, and as Keats puts it, "A thing of beauty is a joy forever."<br /><br />In short, this one is a standard wodehousean fare, which is to say it's funny, sunny, clever, gentle, and optimistic. Like his eponymous hero Uncle Fred, PGW always spreads sweetness and light. Pure win.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15144577320230548432" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1944639836" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1944639836" />
    <a title="Shauna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23595103-shauna"><img alt="Shauna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435999240p2/23595103.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

          </span>

<div id="review_787540418" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/787540418" />
    <a title="Sheila Beaumont" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3303083-sheila-beaumont"><img alt="Sheila Beaumont" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1266970607p2/3303083.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/787540418?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sheila Beaumont" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sheila Beaumont" href="/user/show/3303083-sheila-beaumont">Sheila Beaumont</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393343069" href="/book/show/13707721-uncle-fred-in-the-springtime">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3303083-sheila-beaumont?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3303083-sheila-beaumont?shelf=funny">funny</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer787540418" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13549041453392344454">After laughing my way through this hilarious novel, I'm convinced that this is one of P.G. Wodehouse's funniest books. Of course, I feel that way after reading just about any of his books. But I really do think <i>Uncle Fred in the Springtime</i> is one of his very best. If you enjoy literate prose, eccentric characters, and lots of laughs, don't miss this one!</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2660527098" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2660527098" />
    <a title="Aniruddha Rege" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/84465231-aniruddha-rege"><img alt="Aniruddha Rege" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551505140p2/84465231.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2660527098?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Aniruddha Rege" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Aniruddha Rege" href="/user/show/84465231-aniruddha-rege">Aniruddha Rege</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393343069" href="/book/show/13707721-uncle-fred-in-the-springtime">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2660527098" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8977257457946148951">An Amateur’s review of the Master:<br/><br/>Uncle Fred in the Springtime by P.G. Wodehouse<br/><br/><br/>A wise man once said, or should have said in his infinite wisdom, that the only true cure to feeling down is to pick up a Wodehouse and read it cover to cover. If that doesn’t sort the problem right out, the trick is to pick up another Wodehouse, and repeat the blasted exercise. If at the end of it all, you aren’t smiling from ear to ear, please do enjoy the heavens when you reach there.<br/><br/>From this first passage, you m</span>
  <span id="freeText8977257457946148951" style="display:none">An Amateur’s review of the Master:<br /><br />Uncle Fred in the Springtime by P.G. Wodehouse<br /><br /><br />A wise man once said, or should have said in his infinite wisdom, that the only true cure to feeling down is to pick up a Wodehouse and read it cover to cover. If that doesn’t sort the problem right out, the trick is to pick up another Wodehouse, and repeat the blasted exercise. If at the end of it all, you aren’t smiling from ear to ear, please do enjoy the heavens when you reach there.<br /><br />From this first passage, you must be well aware by now that I am something of a Wodehouse fanboy. You will also realize, as you go along, that this isn’t as much a review as it is a tribute to the only man on earth (you’ll see at the end why I refer to him in an inappropriate tense) I am jealous of. P.G. Wodehouse, in my opinion, is the finest writer English has ever had the honor of hosting in its hall of greatness.<br /><br />The reasons I have for making a statement so ballsy and overt, something Wodehouse himself would look upon sternly and with an eye which Aunts in his world are prone to giving erring nephews, are sound and based on fact. You see, he is a simple writer. And I say that as the greatest of compliments. There are no deep metaphors running through his stories. There are no life-changing chapters which would forever alter how you perceive the world. There is no crying involved (unless, of course, there is a song sung in the most toneless of voices and in the most public of arenas à la Sonny Boy) that would cleanse your soul thoroughly. But, when you read what the man has put down on paper, in some of the most beautiful language, adorned by some of the most gorgeous words existing in that language and all ordered in a way which would hitherto seem impossible to mere mortals, you realize, instinctively, that this is the most complex of authors and what you’re reading as a simple, fast-paced tale of unfortunate events and comedic errors, is one of the most difficult things to conceive.<br /><br />The strength of Wodehouse lies more in the language than the plot. I must remember, at this point, that I am reviewing a book and not an author, but it becomes very difficult to separate any single piece of work that Wodehouse has put out from the expansive and utterly mind-blowing body of art that he amassed. Uncle Fred in the Springtime is no exception. It occupies the same pantheon of superlative literature as any Jeeves story you may pick or the choicest of Blandings tales that you may challenge it with or even the most subtle of Psmith (the P is silent) escapades that you may recall. The language is the literal equivalent of that seven food gourmet meal cooked by Anatole and looked forward to by the best men in England. It is delicious. And you can never have enough of it. Eloquent words stringed together into elegant phrases all coming together to form the most mouth-watering chapter you would have the pleasure of consuming is how I would describe this book (and indeed, most of Wodehouse’s books) and still fall short of what I truly want to express.<br /><br />The most difficult achievement for any author is to inspire emotion in his readers. The great authors instill us with fear, thrill, sorrow, grief and in the rare cases, with relief. But only those gifted above all else can bring a smile to those who read their words and perhaps, if they’re lucky, an occasional chuckle at an especially clever play of words. Wodehouse, then, must be truly blessed, because he has the pleasure of inducing in his audience a steady stream of laughter which cannot be held in, even in the most polite of societies. That is his crowning achievement. That he can play with words at will and mould them to his will only shows the mastery he enjoys over the language and we can only thank him that he has employed this mastery to make us laugh (the other author of such absolute greatness chose to give us tragedies aplenty).<br /><br />The language, though blinding in it’s obvious greatness, is not all that makes Wodehouse so enduring. I feel that while the language is his sword, his skill of world-building and plot-building are his subtle daggers, always working silently and killing without anyone realizing it. Uncle Fred in the Springtime is possibly the best testament to this. For a comedy story, the tale is so layered and ridden with multiple characters posing as multiple other characters, that the reader is bound to get his head fogged up and find himself in desperate need of a good ol’ Game of Thrones books, which he would no doubt find much simpler to comprehend. The number of plotlines and characters in this book are truly mind-boggling. The story is so fast and full of suspense that it would give Sidney Sheldon a run for his money, if it came to that. That it is set in Blandings, a world which each of us wish we born into, with all its many characters, idyllic lifestyle, stiff butlers, discerning secretaries and characters constantly falling in and out of love, often with hilarious missteps along the way, makes it all the more jarring for the reader to keep abreast of the latest developments, while simultaneously trying to hold back laughter lest it disturb the rest of the class. That Wodehouse manages to not only take all these plotlines and tie them into neat, happy bow at the end of the rollercoaster are a testament to his skill with the daggers in addition to his glory with the sword (as if creation of characters like Jeeves, Spode, Wooster, Emsworth, Galahead, Psmith, Ukridge etc. etc. etc. weren’t enough exhibits to prove this point in any court of law). That all of the stories have a happy ending, perhaps with two lovers reuniting after a series of misunderstandings or the eternal bachelors saved from the doom of marriage through poor judgement, is no coincidence. There is no place, as Stephen Fry once wrote, for grief in the world of Wodehouse.<br /><br />This hasn’t been much of a book review, I realize. But like any reader worth his salt, I wholeheartedly recommend this book (and indeed, most other Wodehouse books) to anyone in need of a little pick-me-up or even anyone who has spent far too much time reading the deepest of books and getting lost in their own minds. Those are the books which are easy to recognize as great. They are the ones which will survive through ages, always giving something new to each generation.<br /><br />But Wodehouse is a different animal playing a different game. Other authors survive the generations through their books. Wodehouse’s books will survive the generations through him. He is the one who will lend them immortality.<br /><br />He won’t give every generation something new. He will give each generation the exact same thing he gave every other. He will give them a work of absolute genius disguised in the form a light-hearted comedy, putting a smile on every person, across space and time, capable of appreciating the English language, a hearty chuckle and a lasting smile.<br /><br />That’s true genius. And that’s enough.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8977257457946148951" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1162334571737294686">A lovely way to start the year with the hilarious Blanding Castle characters. Connie’s friend the Duke of Dunstable threatens to take the empress pig from her infatuated owner Lord Emsworth. There is also the issue of Ricky the poet trying to get £250 to buy an onion soup bar so he can marry the lovely Polly. There is also the fact that Pongo needs £250 to pay off some gambling debts. Thankfully Lord Ikenham assists his nephew to get the readies through being an imposter and confidence man. <br/><br/>This</span>
  <span id="freeText1162334571737294686" style="display:none">A lovely way to start the year with the hilarious Blanding Castle characters. Connie’s friend the Duke of Dunstable threatens to take the empress pig from her infatuated owner Lord Emsworth. There is also the issue of Ricky the poet trying to get £250 to buy an onion soup bar so he can marry the lovely Polly. There is also the fact that Pongo needs £250 to pay off some gambling debts. Thankfully Lord Ikenham assists his nephew to get the readies through being an imposter and confidence man. <br /><br />This is one of the funniest P G Wodehouse books I have read. A pig in a bathroom, trigger happy son Lord Bosham and overhearing conversations and proceeding to get the wrong end of the stick. Hilarity ensues. </span>

<div id="review_239621674" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/239621674" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3447162-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528489771p2/3447162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/239621674?book_show_action=true">Nov 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/3447162-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9780257663913" href="/book/show/2289595.Uncle_Fred_In_The_Springtime">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer239621674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13465285521240329152">I am amazed that Wodehouse finds so many reasons for young couples to break their engagements, as well as reasons for needing to raise a bit of capital. The last paragraph of this novel seems to convey a warm, fuzzy feeling, but there's really not as much to be warm and fuzzy about as in the other Wodehouses I've read. They usually end with about six weddings in the works. This one has only one that we can be relatively sure of happening - that of Polly and Ricky - and her father and his uncle a</span>
  <span id="freeText13465285521240329152" style="display:none">I am amazed that Wodehouse finds so many reasons for young couples to break their engagements, as well as reasons for needing to raise a bit of capital. The last paragraph of this novel seems to convey a warm, fuzzy feeling, but there's really not as much to be warm and fuzzy about as in the other Wodehouses I've read. They usually end with about six weddings in the works. This one has only one that we can be relatively sure of happening - that of Polly and Ricky - and her father and his uncle are still in the dark about it. We don't know what will happen with Valerie and Horace, and poor Pongo lusts after Polly, but will not win her. Usually there is an equal number of young men and women that can all be matched up satisfactorily. I also felt like I would like to see a bit more of the pig, after her cameo appearance coming out of the bathroom and crashing through the Duke's room. Put me among the pigs!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13465285521240329152" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_320289444" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/320289444" />
    <a title="Zedder" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21571-zedder"><img alt="Zedder" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1173468039p2/21571.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/320289444?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zedder" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zedder" href="/user/show/21571-zedder">Zedder</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer320289444" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13774812852094450884">Before reading "Leave it to Psmith," I had an irrational aversion to non-Jeeves and Wooster Wodehouses. The character of Psmith completely overcame my aversion--he was some sort of unbelievable mongrel of J&W, simultaneously smart + stupid and sensible + debauched, as well as being the most hyper-articulate Wodehouse character I've come across so far. Now having read "Uncle Fred," I can happily report that it isn't just great characters that you can find in non-J&Ws, it's great plots as well. Th</span>
  <span id="freeText13774812852094450884" style="display:none">Before reading "Leave it to Psmith," I had an irrational aversion to non-Jeeves and Wooster Wodehouses. The character of Psmith completely overcame my aversion--he was some sort of unbelievable mongrel of J&amp;W, simultaneously smart + stupid and sensible + debauched, as well as being the most hyper-articulate Wodehouse character I've come across so far. Now having read "Uncle Fred," I can happily report that it isn't just great characters that you can find in non-J&amp;Ws, it's great plots as well. This probably has the most ridiculously fun plot I've come across so far, as well as the richest variety of characters. Additionally, he character of Uncle Fred may well be the most likeable Wodehouse character yet. It's no surprise that Wodehouse himself liked this book so much. Thanks for the recommendation, P. G. W.!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13774812852094450884" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Keith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Keith" href="/user/show/659583-keith">Keith</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393343069" href="/book/show/13707721-uncle-fred-in-the-springtime">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/659583-keith?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/659583-keith?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/659583-keith?shelf=2018">2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2619315391" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9459196625487815275">A defining characteristic of P.G. Wodehouse's marvelous stories is that of the scheme, often a truly complex scheme. While I have not read the entire Wodehouse corpus I venture a guess that <i>Uncle Fred in the Springtime</i> is a sure contender for the most complicated scheme of all. Like many of Wodehouse's tales it involves love, marriage, and money. But how this happy conclusion is reached can only be revealed within its pages. A happy read. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_329668631" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/329668631" />
    <a title="Elisabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6625371-elisabeth"><img alt="Elisabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565618963p2/6625371.jpg" /></a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('+oBmtXEROhgdvuyCBVaQUqhYQfFrBnvHRDsIUQqNy8su8fwSLDVcFJS2Ft9iPoh4RbuTyOIHmTS1h7a82w6pjw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7963.P_G_Wodehouse">About P.G. Wodehouse</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7963.P_G_Wodehouse"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1198684105p3/7963.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7963.P_G_Wodehouse">P.G. Wodehouse
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
5,399
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15884221829725715270">Sir Pelham Grenville Wodehouse, KBE, was a comic writer who enjoyed enormous popular success during a career of more than seventy years and continues to be widely read over 40 years after his death. Despite the political and social upheavals that occurred during his life, much of which was spent in France and the United States, Wodehouse's main canvas remained that of prewar English upper-class so</span>
  <span id="freeText15884221829725715270" style="display:none">Sir Pelham Grenville Wodehouse, KBE, was a comic writer who enjoyed enormous popular success during a career of more than seventy years and continues to be widely read over 40 years after his death. Despite the political and social upheavals that occurred during his life, much of which was spent in France and the United States, Wodehouse's main canvas remained that of prewar English upper-class society, reflecting his birth, education, and youthful writing career.<br /><br />An acknowledged master of English prose, Wodehouse has been admired both by contemporaries such as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4025.Hilaire_Belloc" title="Hilaire Belloc" rel="nofollow noopener">Hilaire Belloc</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11315.Evelyn_Waugh" title="Evelyn Waugh" rel="nofollow noopener">Evelyn Waugh</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6989.Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling" rel="nofollow noopener">Rudyard Kipling</a> and by more recent writers such as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4.Douglas_Adams" title="Douglas Adams" rel="nofollow noopener">Douglas Adams</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3299.Salman_Rushdie" title="Salman Rushdie" rel="nofollow noopener">Salman Rushdie</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett" title="Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Terry Pratchett</a>. Sean O'Casey famously called him "English literature's performing flea", a description that Wodehouse used as the title of a collection of his letters to a friend, Bill Townend.<br /><br />Best known today for the <i>Jeeves</i> and <i>Blandings Castle</i> novels and short stories, Wodehouse was also a talented playwright and lyricist who was part author and writer of fifteen plays and of 250 lyrics for some thirty musical comedies. He worked with Cole Porter on the musical <i>Anything Goes</i> (1934) and frequently collaborated with Jerome Kern and Guy Bolton. He wrote the lyrics for the hit song <i>Bill</i> in Kern's Show Boat (1927), wrote the lyrics for the Gershwin/Romberg musical <i>Rosalie</i> (1928), and collaborated with Rudolf Friml on a musical version of <i>The Three Musketeers</i> (1928).</span>
  <a data-text-id="15884221829725715270" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
